</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Korea-Jejudo-Coast-03.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Korea-Jejudo-Coast-03.jpg/220px-Korea-Jejudo-Coast-03.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Korea-Jejudo-Coast-03.jpg/330px-Korea-Jejudo-Coast-03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Korea-Jejudo-Coast-03.jpg/440px-Korea-Jejudo-Coast-03.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

</div>
</div>
<p>Korea is located on the <a href="/wiki/Korean_Peninsula" title="Korean Peninsula">Korean Peninsula</a> in <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a>. To the northwest, the Amnok River separates Korea from China and to the northeast, the Duman River separates Korea from China and Russia. The peninsula is surrounded by the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_Sea" title="Yellow Sea">Yellow Sea</a> to the west, the <a href="/wiki/East_China_Sea" title="East China Sea">East China Sea</a> and Korea Strait to the south, and the East Sea <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=(Sea_of_Japan)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="(Sea of Japan) (page does not exist)">(Sea of Japan)</a> .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[158]</a></sup> Notable islands include <a href="/wiki/Jeju_Island" title="Jeju Island">Jeju Island</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ulleung_Island" title="Ulleung Island">Ulleung Island</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dokdo" title="Dokdo">Dokdo</a>.</p>
<p>The southern and western parts of the peninsula have well-developed plains, while the eastern and northern parts are mountainous. The highest mountain in Korea is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mount_Paektu" title="Mount Paektu">Mount Paektu</a> (2,744 m), through which runs the border with China. The southern extension of Mount Paektu is a highland called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaema_Heights" title="Gaema Heights">Gaema Heights</a>. This highland was mainly raised during the <a href="/wiki/Cenozoic" title="Cenozoic">Cenozoic</a> orogeny and partly covered by volcanic matter. To the south of Gaema Gowon, successive high mountains are located along the eastern coast of the peninsula. This mountain range is named <a href="/wiki/Baekdudaegan" title="Baekdudaegan">Baekdudaegan</a>. Some significant mountains include <a href="/wiki/Sobaeksan" title="Sobaeksan">Mount Sobaek</a> or Sobaeksan (1,439 m), <a href="/wiki/Mount_Kumgang" title="Mount Kumgang">Mount Kumgang</a> (1,638 m), <a href="/wiki/Seoraksan" title="Seoraksan">Mount Seorak</a> (1,708 m), <a href="/wiki/Taebaeksan" title="Taebaeksan">Mount Taebaek</a> (1,567 m), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mount_Jiri" title="Mount Jiri">Mount Jiri</a> (1,915 m). There are several lower, secondary mountain series whose direction is almost perpendicular to that of <a href="/wiki/Baekdudaegan" title="Baekdudaegan">Baekdudaegan</a>. They are developed along the tectonic line of Mesozoic orogeny and their directions are basically northwest.</p>
<p>Unlike most ancient mountains on the mainland, many important islands in Korea were formed by volcanic activity in the Cenozoic orogeny. Jeju Island, situated off the southern coast, is a large volcanic island whose main mountain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mount_Halla" title="Mount Halla">Mount Halla</a> or Hallasan (1950 m) is the highest in South Korea. Ulleung Island is a volcanic island in the East Sea, whose composition is more felsic than Jeju-do. The volcanic islands tend to be younger, the more westward.</p>
<p>Because the mountainous region is mostly on the eastern part of the peninsula, the main <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rivers_of_Korea" title="Rivers of Korea">rivers</a> tend to flow westwards. Two exceptions are the southward-flowing <a href="/wiki/Nakdong_River" title="Nakdong River">Nakdong River</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seomjin_River" title="Seomjin River">Seomjin River</a>. Important rivers running westward include the Amnok River, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ch%27ongch%27on_River" title="Ch'ongch'on River">Chongchon River</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Taedong_River" title="Taedong River">Taedong River</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Han_River_(Korea)" title="Han River (Korea)">Han River</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Geum_River" title="Geum River">Geum River</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Yeongsan_River" title="Yeongsan River">Yeongsan River</a>. These rivers have vast flood plains and provide an ideal environment for wet-rice cultivation.</p>
<p>The southern and southwestern coastlines of Korea form a well-developed <a href="/wiki/Ria" title="Ria">ria</a> coastline, known as <i>Dadohae-jin</i> in Korean. Its convoluted coastline provides mild seas, and the resulting calm environment allows for safe navigation, fishing, and seaweed farming. In addition to the complex coastline, the western coast of the Korean Peninsula has an extremely high tidal amplitude (at <a href="/wiki/Incheon" title="Incheon">Incheon</a>, around the middle of the western coast. It can get as high as 9 m). Vast tidal flats have been developing on the south and west coastlines.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Wildlife">Wildlife</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Wildlife">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_of_Korea" title="Wildlife of Korea">Wildlife of Korea</a></div>
<p>Animal life of Korea includes a considerable number of bird species and native <a href="/wiki/Freshwater_fish" title="Freshwater fish">freshwater fish</a>. Native or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endemic" title="Endemic">endemic</a> species of the <a href="/wiki/Korean_Peninsula" title="Korean Peninsula">Korean Peninsula</a> include <a href="/wiki/Korean_hare" title="Korean hare">Korean hare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_deer" title="Water deer">Korean water deer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korean_field_mouse" title="Korean field mouse">Korean field mouse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korean_brown_frog" title="Korean brown frog">Korean brown frog</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Korean_pine" title="Korean pine">Korean pine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Picea_koraiensis" title="Picea koraiensis">Korean spruce</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Korean_Demilitarized_Zone" title="Korean Demilitarized Zone">Korean Demilitarized Zone</a> (DMZ) with its forest and natural <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wetlands" title="Wetlands">wetlands</a> is a unique biodiversity spot, which harbours eighty two <a href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">endangered species</a>.</p>
<p>There are also approximately 3,034 species of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vascular_plants" title="Vascular plants">vascular plants</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Koreans" title="Koreans">Koreans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_South_Korea" title="Demographics of South Korea">Demographics of South Korea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_North_Korea" title="Demographics of North Korea">Demographics of North Korea</a></div>
<p>The combined population of the Koreans is about 76 million (North Korea: 25 million, South Korea: 51 million). Korea is chiefly populated by a highly <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/homogeneous" title="wikt:homogeneous">homogeneous</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnic" title="Ethnic">ethnic</a> group, the <a href="/wiki/Koreans" title="Koreans">Koreans</a>, who speak the <a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean language</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[159]</a></sup> The number of foreigners living in Korea has also steadily increased since the late 20th century, particularly in South Korea, where more than 1 million foreigners reside.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[160]</a></sup> It was estimated in 2006 that only 26,700 of the old <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnic_Chinese_in_Korea" title="Ethnic Chinese in Korea">Chinese community</a> now remain in South Korea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[161]</a></sup> However, in recent years, immigration from mainland China has increased; 624,994 persons of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China">Chinese</a> nationality have immigrated to South Korea, including 443,566 of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnic_Koreans_in_China" title="Ethnic Koreans in China">ethnic Korean</a> descent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-million_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-million-163">[162]</a></sup> Small communities of ethnic Chinese and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_people_in_North_Korea" title="Japanese people in North Korea">Japanese</a> are also found in North Korea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[163]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean language</a> and <a href="/wiki/Korean_Sign_Language" title="Korean Sign Language">Korean Sign Language</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hunmin_jeong-eum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="492" data-file-width="425" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Hunmin_jeong-eum.jpg/170px-Hunmin_jeong-eum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Hunmin_jeong-eum.jpg/255px-Hunmin_jeong-eum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Hunmin_jeong-eum.jpg/340px-Hunmin_jeong-eum.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hunmin_jeong-eum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Hunminjeongeum" title="Hunminjeongeum">Hunminjeongeum</a></i>, afterwards called <a href="/wiki/Hangul" title="Hangul">Hangul</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean</a> is the official language of both North and South Korea, and (along with Mandarin) of <a href="/wiki/Yanbian_Korean_Autonomous_Prefecture" title="Yanbian Korean Autonomous Prefecture">Yanbian Korean Autonomous Prefecture</a> in the Manchuria area of China. Worldwide, there are up to 80 million speakers of the Korean language. South Korea has around 50 million speakers while North Korea around 25 million. Other large groups of Korean speakers through <a href="/wiki/Korean_diaspora" title="Korean diaspora">Korean diaspora</a> are found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China">China</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, former <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and elsewhere.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Korean_language#Classification" title="Korean language">classification of Korean</a> is debated. Some linguists place it in the <a href="/wiki/Altaic_languages" title="Altaic languages">Altaic</a> language family; others consider it to be a <a href="/wiki/Language_isolate" title="Language isolate">language isolate</a>. Korean is <a href="/wiki/Agglutinative_language" title="Agglutinative language">agglutinative</a> in its morphology and <a href="/wiki/Subject%E2%80%93object%E2%80%93verb" title="Subject–object–verb">SOV</a> in its <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">syntax</a>. Like <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_language" title="Vietnamese language">Vietnamese</a>, Korean has borrowed much vocabulary from the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a> or created vocabulary on Chinese models.</p>
<p>Modern Korean is written almost exclusively in the script of the <a href="/wiki/Hangul" title="Hangul">Korean alphabet</a> (known as Hangul in South Korea and Chosungul in China and North Korea), which was invented in the 15th century. Korean is sometimes written with the addition of some Chinese characters called Hanja; however, this is only occasionally seen nowadays. While Hangul may appear <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Logographic" title="Logographic">logographic</a>, it is actually a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phonemic" title="Phonemic">phonemic</a> alphabet organised into <a href="/wiki/Syllable" title="Syllable">syllabic</a> blocks. Each block consists of at least two of the 24 hangul letters (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jamo" title="Jamo">jamo</a></i>): at least one each of the 14 <a href="/wiki/Consonant" title="Consonant">consonants</a> and 10 <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">vowels</a>. Historically, the alphabet had several additional letters (see <a href="/wiki/Hangul#Obsolete_jamo" title="Hangul">obsolete jamo</a>). For a phonological description of the letters, see <a href="/wiki/Korean_phonology" title="Korean phonology">Korean phonology</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture_and_arts">Culture and arts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Culture and arts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Korea" title="Culture of Korea">Culture of Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korean_art" title="Korean art">Korean art</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korean_pottery_and_porcelain" title="Korean pottery and porcelain">Korean pottery and porcelain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korean_martial_arts" title="Korean martial arts">Korean martial arts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korean_dance" title="Korean dance">Korean dance</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Korean_bow" title="Korean bow">Korean bow</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Korean_architecture" title="Korean architecture">Korean architecture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Korea_south_kangnung_kyongpodae.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="218" data-file-width="334" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Korea_south_kangnung_kyongpodae.jpg/220px-Korea_south_kangnung_kyongpodae.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Korea_south_kangnung_kyongpodae.jpg/330px-Korea_south_kangnung_kyongpodae.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8f/Korea_south_kangnung_kyongpodae.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Korea_south_kangnung_kyongpodae.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Korean Buddhist architecture</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Korean_sword_dance-Jinju_geommu-03.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Korean_sword_dance-Jinju_geommu-03.jpg/220px-Korean_sword_dance-Jinju_geommu-03.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Korean_sword_dance-Jinju_geommu-03.jpg/330px-Korean_sword_dance-Jinju_geommu-03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Korean_sword_dance-Jinju_geommu-03.jpg/440px-Korean_sword_dance-Jinju_geommu-03.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Korean_sword_dance-Jinju_geommu-03.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Traditional Korean dance (<i>Jinju geommu</i>)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In ancient Chinese texts, Korea is referred to as "Rivers and Mountains Embroidered on Silk" (<span lang="ko" xml:lang="ko">금수강산</span>, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%8C%A6" title="wikt:錦">錦</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E7%B9%A1" title="wikt:繡">繡</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%B1%9F" title="wikt:江">江</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%B1%B1" title="wikt:山">山</a>) and "Eastern Nation of Decorum" (<span lang="ko" xml:lang="ko">동방예의지국</span>, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%9D%B1" title="wikt:東">東</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%96%B9" title="wikt:方">方</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E7%A6%AE" title="wikt:禮">禮</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%84%80" title="wikt:儀">儀</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E4%B9%8B" title="wikt:之">之</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%9C%8B" title="wikt:國">國</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[164]</a></sup> Individuals are regarded as one year old when they are born, as Koreans reckon the pregnancy period as one year of life for infants, and age increments increase on <a href="/wiki/Korean_New_Year" title="Korean New Year">New Year's Day</a> rather than on the anniversary of birthdays. Thus, one born immediately before New Year's Day may only be a few days old in western reckoning, but two years old in Korea. Accordingly, a Korean person's stated age (at least among fellow Koreans) will be one or two years more than their age according to western reckoning. However, western reckoning is sometimes applied with regard to the concept of <a href="/wiki/Legal_age" title="Legal age">legal age</a>; for example, the <a href="/wiki/Legal_drinking_age" title="Legal drinking age">legal age for purchasing alcohol</a> or <a href="/wiki/Smoking_age" title="Smoking age">cigarettes</a> in the Republic of Korea is 19, which is measured according to western reckoning.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Korean_literature" title="Korean literature">Korean literature</a></div>
<p>Korean literature written before the end of the Joseon Dynasty is called "Classical" or "Traditional." Literature, written in Chinese characters (<a href="/wiki/Hanja" title="Hanja">hanja</a>), was established at the same time as the Chinese script arrived on the peninsula. Korean scholars were writing poetry in the classical Korean style as early as the 2nd century <span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span>, reflecting Korean thoughts and experiences of that time. Classical Korean literature has its roots in traditional folk beliefs and folk tales of the peninsula, strongly influenced by <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a>.</p>
<p>Modern literature is often linked with the development of <a href="/wiki/Hangul" title="Hangul">hangul</a>, which helped spread literacy from the aristocracy to the common men and women. Hangul, however, only reached a dominant position in Korean literature in the second half of the 19th century, resulting in a major growth in Korean literature. <i>Sinsoseol</i>, for instance, are novels written in hangul.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a> led to the development of literature centered on the wounds and chaos of <a href="/wiki/War" title="War">war</a>. Much of the post-war literature in South Korea deals with the daily lives of ordinary people, and their struggles with national pain. The collapse of the traditional Korean value system is another common theme of the time.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Korea" title="Music of Korea">Music of Korea</a></div>
<p>Traditional Korean music includes combinations of the folk, vocal, religious and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Korean_Court_Music" title="Korean Court Music">ritual</a> music styles of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Korean_people" title="Korean people">Korean people</a>. Korean music has been practiced since prehistoric times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[165]</a></sup> Korean music falls into two broad categories. The first, <a href="/wiki/Hyangak" title="Hyangak">Hyangak</a>, literally means <i>The local music</i> or <i>Music native to Korea</i> of which example is <i>Sujecheon</i>, a piece of instrumental music as old as 1,300 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[166]</a></sup> The second, <i>yangak</i>, represent a more Western style.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Korea" title="Religion in Korea">Religion in Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_South_Korea" title="Religion in South Korea">Religion in South Korea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_North_Korea" title="Religion in North Korea">Religion in North Korea</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Korean_shamanism" title="Korean shamanism">Korean shamanism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korean_Confucianism" title="Korean Confucianism">Korean Confucianism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korean_Buddhism" title="Korean Buddhism">Korean Buddhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taoism_in_Korea" title="Taoism in Korea">Taoism in Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Korea" title="Christianity in Korea">Christianity in Korea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Korea" title="Islam in Korea">Islam in Korea</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Goryeo_Buddhist_painting.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="366" height="279" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Goryeo_Buddhist_painting.jpg/170px-Goryeo_Buddhist_painting.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Goryeo_Buddhist_painting.jpg/255px-Goryeo_Buddhist_painting.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Goryeo_Buddhist_painting.jpg/340px-Goryeo_Buddhist_painting.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Goryeo_Buddhist_painting.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Amitabha and Eight Great Bodhisattvas</i>, Goryeo scroll from the 1300s</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confucian" title="Confucian">Confucian</a> tradition has dominated Korean thought, along with contributions by <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Korean_Shamanism" title="Korean Shamanism">Korean Shamanism</a>. Since the middle of the 20th century, however, <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> has competed with Buddhism in South Korea, while religious practice has been suppressed in North Korea. Throughout Korean history and culture, regardless of separation; the influence of traditional beliefs of Korean Shamanism, Mahayana Buddhism, Confucianism and Taoism have remained an underlying religion of the Korean people as well as a vital aspect of their culture; all these traditions have coexisted peacefully for hundreds of years up to today despite strong Westernisation from <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">Christian</a> missionary conversions in the South<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[169]</a></sup> or the pressure from the <a href="/wiki/Juche" title="Juche">Juche</a> <a href="/wiki/Government_of_North_Korea" title="Government of North Korea">government</a> in the North.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[171]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to 2005 statistics compiled by the South Korean government, about 46% of citizens profess to follow no particular religion. <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Korea" title="Christianity in Korea">Christians</a> account for 29.2% of the population (of which are Protestants 18.3% and Catholics 10.9%) and <a href="/wiki/Korean_Buddhism" title="Korean Buddhism">Buddhists</a> 22.8%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[172]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islam_in_South_Korea" title="Islam in South Korea">Islam in South Korea</a> is practiced by about 45,000 natives (about 0.09% of the population) in addition to some 100,000 foreign workers from Muslim countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[173]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Cuisine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Korean_cuisine" title="Korean cuisine">Korean cuisine</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Korean_tea_ceremony" title="Korean tea ceremony">Korean tea ceremony</a> and <a href="/wiki/Korean_royal_court_cuisine" title="Korean royal court cuisine">Korean royal court cuisine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tteokbokki.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4032" data-file-width="6048" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Tteokbokki.JPG/220px-Tteokbokki.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Tteokbokki.JPG/330px-Tteokbokki.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Tteokbokki.JPG/440px-Tteokbokki.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tteokbokki.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tteokbokki" title="Tteokbokki">Tteokbokki</a>, rice cakes with spicy gochujang sauce.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Koreans traditionally believe that the taste and quality of food depend on its spices and sauces, the essential ingredients to making a delicious meal. Therefore, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soybean_paste" title="Soybean paste">soybean paste</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soy_sauce" title="Soy sauce">soy sauce</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Gochujang" title="Gochujang">gochujang</a></i> or red pepper paste and <a href="/wiki/Kimchi" title="Kimchi">kimchi</a> are some of the most important staples in a Korean household.</p>
<p>Korean cuisine was greatly influenced by the geography and climate of the Korean Peninsula, which is known for its cold autumns and winters, therefore there are many fermented dishes and hot soups and stews.</p>
<p>Korean cuisine is probably best known for <a href="/wiki/Kimchi" title="Kimchi">kimchi</a>, a side dish which uses a distinctive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(food)" title="Fermentation (food)">fermentation</a> process of preserving vegetables, most commonly cabbage. Kimchi is said to relieve the pores on the skin, thereby reducing wrinkles and providing nutrients to the skin naturally. It is also healthy, as it provides necessary vitamins and nutrients. Gochujang, a traditional Korean sauce made of red pepper is also commonly used, often as pepper (chilli) paste, earning the cuisine a reputation for spiciness.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bulgogi" title="Bulgogi">Bulgogi</a> (roasted marinated meat, usually beef), <a href="/wiki/Galbi" title="Galbi">galbi</a> (marinated grilled short ribs), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samgyeopsal" title="Samgyeopsal">samgyeopsal</a> (pork belly) are popular meat entrees. Fish is also a popular commodity, as it is the traditional meat that Koreans eat. Meals are usually accompanied by a soup or stew, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galbitang" title="Galbitang">galbitang</a> (stewed ribs) or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doenjang_jjigae" title="Doenjang jjigae">doenjang jjigae</a> (fermented bean paste soup). The center of the table is filled with a shared collection of sidedishes called <a href="/wiki/Banchan" title="Banchan">banchan</a>.</p>
<p>Other popular dishes include <i><a href="/wiki/Bibimbap" title="Bibimbap">bibimbap</a></i> which literally means "mixed rice" (rice mixed with meat, vegetables, and red pepper paste) and <a href="/wiki/Naengmyeon" title="Naengmyeon">naengmyeon</a> (cold noodles).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[175]</a></sup></p>
<p>Instant noodles or <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ramyeon" title="Ramyeon">ramyeon</a></i> are a popular snack food and Koreans also enjoy food from <i><a href="/wiki/Pojangmacha" title="Pojangmacha">pojangmachas</a></i> (street vendors), where customers can buy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tteokbokki" title="Tteokbokki">tteokbokki</a> (rice cake and fish cake with a spicy gochujang sauce), <i><a href="/wiki/Gimbap" title="Gimbap">gimbap</a></i> made of steamed white rice wrapped in dried <a href="/wiki/Laver_(seaweed)" title="Laver (seaweed)">laver seaweed</a> as well as fried squid and glazed sweet potato. <a href="/wiki/Sundae_(Korean_food)" title="Sundae (Korean food)">Soondae</a>, a sausage made of cellophane noodles and pork blood, is widely eaten.</p>
<p>Additionally, some other common snacks include "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Choco_Pie" title="Choco Pie">Choco Pie</a>", shrimp crackers, "bbeongtwigi" (puffed rice grains), and "nurungji" (slightly burnt rice). Nurungji can be eaten as it is or boiled with water to make a soup. Nurungji can also be eaten as a snack or a dessert.</p>
<p>Korea is unique among Asian countries in its use of metal chopsticks. Metal chopsticks have been discovered in archaeological sites belonging to the ancient Korean kingdoms of Goguryeo, Baekje and Silla.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Television">Television</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Television">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Korean television dramas ("K-dramas") have become popular in many countries, and as a result outdoor locations featured in K-dramas have become popular stops for international tourists. Product placements in the dramas have proven effective in advertising; for example, sales of cosmetics, clothing and food favored by the female lead played by actress <a href="/wiki/Jun_Ji-hyun" title="Jun Ji-hyun">Jun Ji-hyun</a> in the drama <a href="/wiki/My_Love_from_the_Star" title="My Love from the Star">My Love from the Star</a> rose significantly after the relevant episodes aired. In one notorious case it was reported that a woman in China became ill after consuming nothing but fried chicken and beer – the character's favorite snack –<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[176]</a></sup> for several days.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_North_Korea" title="Education in North Korea">Education in North Korea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Education_in_South_Korea" title="Education in South Korea">Education in South Korea</a></div>
<p>The modern South Korean school system consists of six years in elementary school, three years in middle school, and three years in high school. Students are required to go to elementary and middle school, and do not have to pay for their education, except for a small fee called a "School Operation Support Fee" that differs from school to school. The <a href="/wiki/Programme_for_International_Student_Assessment" title="Programme for International Student Assessment">Programme for International Student Assessment</a>, coordinated by the <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a>, ranks South Korea's science education as the third best in the world and being significantly higher than the OECD average.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[177]</a></sup></p>
<p>South Korea ranks second on math and literature and first in <span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">problem solving</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="unverified (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>. Although South Korean students often rank high on international comparative assessments, the education system is criticised for emphasising too much upon passive learning and memorization. The South Korean education system is rather notably strict and structured as compared to its counterparts in most Western societies. Also, the prevalence of non-school for-profit private institutes such as academies or cram schools (<a href="/wiki/Hagwon" title="Hagwon">Hagwon</a> [학원]), which too emphasise passive memorisation, as opposed to conceptual understanding, in students are criticised as a major social problem. <span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">After students enter university, however, the situation is markedly reversed</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="biassed, unverifiable and quite likely inaccurate (April 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In Korea, university is hard to enter, and graduation is comparatively easier than entry.</p>
<p>The North Korean education system consists primarily of <a href="/wiki/State_school" title="State school">universal and state funded</a> schooling by the <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_North_Korea" title="Politics of North Korea">government</a>. The national literacy rate for citizens 15 years of age and above is over 99 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-loc-nk_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-loc-nk-179">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[179]</a></sup> Children go through one year of kindergarten, four years of <a href="/wiki/Primary_education" title="Primary education">primary education</a>, six years of <a href="/wiki/Secondary_education" title="Secondary education">secondary education</a>, and then on to <a href="/wiki/University" title="University">universities</a>. The most prestigious university in the DPRK is <a href="/wiki/Kim_Il-sung_University" title="Kim Il-sung University">Kim Il-sung University</a>. Other notable universities include <a href="/wiki/Kim_Chaek_University_of_Technology" title="Kim Chaek University of Technology">Kim Chaek University of Technology</a>, which focuses on computer science, <a href="/wiki/Pyongyang_University_of_Foreign_Studies" title="Pyongyang University of Foreign Studies">Pyongyang University of Foreign Studies</a>, which trains working level diplomats and trade officials, and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Kim_Hyong_Jik_University&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Kim Hyong Jik University (page does not exist)">Kim Hyong Jik University</a>, which trains teachers.</p>
<p>Outside the formal structure of schools and classrooms in the north is the extremely important "social education". This education includes not only <a href="/wiki/Extracurricular_activity" title="Extracurricular activity">extracurricular activities</a> but also family life and the broadest range of <a href="/wiki/Interpersonal_relationship" title="Interpersonal relationship">human relationships</a> within society. There is great sensitivity to the influence of the <a href="/wiki/Social_environment" title="Social environment">social environment</a> on the growing child and its role in the development of his or her character. The ideal of social education is to provide a carefully controlled environment in which <a href="/wiki/Propaganda" title="Propaganda">children are exposed only to</a> pro-<a href="/wiki/Juche" title="Juche">Juche</a> and anti-south influences. According to a North Korean official interviewed in 1990, 'School education is not enough to turn the rising generation into men of knowledge, virtue, and <a href="/wiki/Physical_fitness" title="Physical fitness">physical fitness</a>. After school, our children have many spare hours. So it's important to efficiently organise their afterschool education'.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Science_and_technology">Science and technology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Science and technology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_science_and_technology_in_Korea" title="History of science and technology in Korea">History of science and technology in Korea</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Korean_inventions_and_discoveries" title="List of Korean inventions and discoveries">List of Korean inventions and discoveries</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Korean_book-Jikji-Selected_Teachings_of_Buddhist_Sages_and_Seon_Masters-1377.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="500" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Korean_book-Jikji-Selected_Teachings_of_Buddhist_Sages_and_Seon_Masters-1377.jpg/220px-Korean_book-Jikji-Selected_Teachings_of_Buddhist_Sages_and_Seon_Masters-1377.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Korean_book-Jikji-Selected_Teachings_of_Buddhist_Sages_and_Seon_Masters-1377.jpg/330px-Korean_book-Jikji-Selected_Teachings_of_Buddhist_Sages_and_Seon_Masters-1377.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Korean_book-Jikji-Selected_Teachings_of_Buddhist_Sages_and_Seon_Masters-1377.jpg/440px-Korean_book-Jikji-Selected_Teachings_of_Buddhist_Sages_and_Seon_Masters-1377.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Korean_book-Jikji-Selected_Teachings_of_Buddhist_Sages_and_Seon_Masters-1377.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Jikji" title="Jikji">Jikji</a></i>, <i>Selected Teachings of Buddhist Sages and Seon Masters</i>, the earliest known book printed with movable metal type, 1377. Bibliothèque Nationale de Paris.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>One of the best known artifacts of Korea's history of science and technology is the <a href="/wiki/Cheomseongdae" title="Cheomseongdae">Cheomseongdae</a> (첨성대, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E7%9E%BB" title="wikt:瞻">瞻</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%98%9F" title="wikt:星">星</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%87%BA" title="wikt:臺">臺</a>), a 9.4-meter high observatory built in 634.</p>
<p>The earliest known surviving Korean example of woodblock printing is the <a href="/wiki/Seokgatap#National_treasure_No.126" title="Seokgatap">Mugujeonggwang Great Dharani Sutra</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[180]</a></sup> It is believed to have been printed in Korea in 750–51, which if correct, would make it older than the <a href="/wiki/Diamond_Sutra" title="Diamond Sutra">Diamond Sutra</a>.</p>
<p>During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goryeo_Dynasty" title="Goryeo Dynasty">Goryeo Dynasty</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metal_movable_type" title="Metal movable type">metal movable type</a> printing was invented by <a href="/wiki/Choe_Yun-ui" title="Choe Yun-ui">Choe Yun-ui</a> in 1234.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[183]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[185]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[186]</a></sup> This invention made printing easier, more efficient and also increased literacy, which observed by Chinese visitors was seen to be so important where it was considered to be shameful to not be able to read.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[187]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a> later adopted Korea's movable type printing and spread as far as Central Asia. There is conjecture as to whether or not Choe's invention had any influence on later printing inventions such as Gutenberg's <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">Printing press</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[188]</a></sup> When the <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongols</a> <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasion_of_Europe" title="Mongol invasion of Europe">invaded Europe</a> they inadvertently introduced different kinds of Asian technology.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>During the Joseon period, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turtle_Ship" title="Turtle Ship">Turtle Ship</a> was invented, which were covered by a wooden deck and iron with thorns,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hawley_2005.2C_195f._190-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hawley_2005.2C_195f.-190">[189]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turnbull_2002.2C_244_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-Turnbull_2002.2C_244-191">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roh_2004.2C_13_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roh_2004.2C_13-192">[191]</a></sup> as well as other weapons such as the <a href="/wiki/Korean_cannon" title="Korean cannon">bigyeokjincheolloe cannon</a> (비격진천뢰, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%A3%9B" title="wikt:飛">飛</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%93%8A" title="wikt:擊">擊</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%9C%87" title="wikt:震">震</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%A4%A9" title="wikt:天">天</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%9B%B7" title="wikt:雷">雷</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Hwacha" title="Hwacha">hwacha</a>.</p>
<p>The Korean alphabet <a href="/wiki/Hangul" title="Hangul">hangul</a> was also invented during this time by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sejong_the_Great_of_Joseon" title="Sejong the Great of Joseon">King Sejong the Great</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sport">Sport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Sport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_South_Korea" title="Sport in South Korea">Sport in South Korea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_North_Korea" title="Sport in North Korea">Sport in North Korea</a></div>
<p>While <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">association football</a> remains one of the most popular sports in South Korea, the martial art of <a href="/wiki/Taekwondo" title="Taekwondo">taekwondo</a> is considered to be the national sport. <a href="/wiki/Baseball" title="Baseball">Baseball</a> and <a href="/wiki/Golf" title="Golf">golf</a> are also popular.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taekwondo">Taekwondo</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Taekwondo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Taekwondo" title="Taekwondo">Taekwondo</a></div>
<p>Taekwondo is one of Korea's most famous sports. It combines combat techniques, self-defense, sport, exercise and in some cases meditation and philosophy. Taekwondo has become an official Olympic sport, starting as a demonstration event in <a href="/wiki/1988_Summer_Olympics" title="1988 Summer Olympics">1988</a> (when South Korea hosted the Games in Seoul) and becoming an official medal event in <a href="/wiki/2000_Summer_Olympics" title="2000 Summer Olympics">2000.</a> There are two main authoritative Taekwondo organizations in the world. One is World Taekwondo (formerly World Taekwondo Federation and the larger of the two) and the other is ITF (International Taekwondo Federation).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hapkido">Hapkido</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Hapkido">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hapkido" title="Hapkido">Hapkido</a></div>
<p>Hapkido is a modern <a href="/wiki/Korean_martial_arts" title="Korean martial arts">Korean martial art</a> with a grappling focus that employs joint locks, throws, kicks, punches and other striking attacks like attacks against pressure points. Hapkido emphasizes circular motion, non-resisting movements and control of the opponent. Practitioners seek to gain advantage through footwork and body positioning to employ leverage, avoiding the pure use of strength against strength.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ssireum">Ssireum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Ssireum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ssireum" title="Ssireum">Ssireum</a></div>
<p>Ssireum is a traditional form of wrestling that has been practiced in Korea for thousands of years, with evidence discovered from Goguryeo of Korea's Three Kingdoms Period (57 <span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span> to 688). Ssireum is the traditional national sport of Korea. During a match, opponents grip each other by sash belts wrapped around the waist and the thigh, attempting to throw their competitor to the sandy ground of the ring. The first opponent to touch the ground with any body part above the knee or to lose hold of their opponent loses the round.</p>
<p>Ssireum competitions are traditionally held twice a year, during the <a href="/wiki/Dano_(Korean_festival)" title="Dano (Korean festival)">Dano Festival</a> (the 5th day of the fifth lunar month) and <a href="/wiki/Chuseok" title="Chuseok">Chuseok</a> (the 15th day of the 8th lunar month). Competitions are also held throughout the year as a part of festivals and other events.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_public_holidays_in_South_Korea">Notable public holidays in South Korea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Notable public holidays in South Korea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_public_holidays_in_South_Korea" title="List of public holidays in South Korea">List of public holidays in South Korea</a></div>
<h3><span id="Independence_Movement_Day,_March_1st"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence_Movement_Day.2C_March_1st">Independence Movement Day, March 1st</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Independence Movement Day, March 1st">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Samiljeol" title="Samiljeol">Samiljeol</a>, Independence Movement Day, commemorates Korea's declaration of independence from Japanese occupation on March 1, 1919. The name is derived from Korean 삼 "sam" 'three', 일 "il" 'one,' and 절 "jeol" 'day', the date of the uprising in 1919. Korea was annexed to the Empire of Japan on August 29, 1910 following the imposed Japan-Korea Treaty. On March 1, 1919, Korean presented their resistance towards Japan and Japanese occupation with a declaration of independence. Following the conclusion of World War II, Korea was liberated from Japan and its independence restored. The newly established Korean government set aside March 1 as a national holiday to commemorate the sacrifices borne in the long struggle for Korean independence.</p>
<h3><span id="Memorial_day,_June_6th"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Memorial_day.2C_June_6th">Memorial day, June 6th</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Memorial day, June 6th">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Memorial_Day_(South_Korea)" title="Memorial Day (South Korea)">Hyunchoongil</a> is the national holiday in Korea commemorating those who fought and died for the nation. In August 1948, only a few years after Korea achieved its independence from Japan, the Korean War, in Korea also known as the 6.25 war, broke out between North and South Korea. During this war, approximately 400,000 soldiers and some one million citizens were killed or injured. In 1953, North and South Korea agreed to a cease-fire, and three years later the Korean government established Hyungchoogil to commemorate the soldiers who fought in the Korean War. Subsequent to its establishment, Hyungchoogil was reinterpreted as a day of remembrance for those who died defending Korea in all conflicts, not only during the Korean War.</p>
<h3><span id="National_Liberation_Day,_August_15th"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="National_Liberation_Day.2C_August_15th">National Liberation Day, August 15th</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: National Liberation Day, August 15th">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/National_Liberation_Day_of_Korea" title="National Liberation Day of Korea">Gwangbokjeol</a> is the day for celebrating liberation of the country from Japan in 1945 as well as celebrating the establishment of Korean government in 1948. Gwangbok means "returned light" representing gaining national sovereignty from Japan. It was first declared to be national holiday in 1949 October 1. On this date every year, the president of Korea visits Independence Hall, and invites diplomatic envoys from many countries and all social standings in countries to Cheongwadae (the Blue House, the Korean presidential residence).</p>
<h3><span id="Hangul_Day,_October_9th"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Hangul_Day.2C_October_9th">Hangul Day, October 9th</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Hangul Day, October 9th">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hangul_Day" title="Hangul Day">Hangul Day</a> (also spelled as Hangeul Day) is a day that celebrates the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Hunminjeongeum" title="Hunminjeongeum">Hunminjeongeum</a> (<a href="/wiki/Hangul" title="Hangul">Hangul</a>, Korean alphabet), which was inscribed to the <a href="/wiki/Memory_of_the_World_Register_%E2%80%93_Asia_and_the_Pacific" title="Memory of the World Register – Asia and the Pacific">UNESCO Memory of the World Register</a> in 1997.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[192]</a></sup> Hangul was created by <a href="/wiki/Sejong_the_Great" title="Sejong the Great">Sejong the Great</a> in 1443 and proclaimed in 1446. Before the creation of Hangul, people in Korea (known as <a href="/wiki/Joseon" title="Joseon">Joseon</a> at the time) primarily wrote using <a href="/wiki/Classical_Chinese" title="Classical Chinese">Classical Chinese</a> alongside native phonetic writing systems that predate Hangul by hundreds of years, including <a href="/wiki/Idu_script" title="Idu script">idu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hyangchal" title="Hyangchal">hyangchal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gugyeol" title="Gugyeol">gugyeol</a>, and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Gakpil&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Gakpil (page does not exist)">gakpil</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[193]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[194]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phonetic_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-phonetic-196">[195]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[196]</a></sup> However, due to the fundamental differences between the Korean and Chinese languages, and the large number of characters needed to be learned, there was much difficulty in learning how to write using Chinese characters for the lower classes, who often didn't have the privilege of education. To assuage this problem, King Sejong created the unique alphabet known as Hangul to promote literacy among the common people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[197]</a></sup> Hangul Day was founded in 1926 during the <a href="/wiki/Korea_under_Japanese_rule" title="Korea under Japanese rule">Japanese occupation</a> by members of the <a href="/wiki/Korean_Language_Society" title="Korean Language Society">Korean Language Society</a>, whose goal was to preserve the Korean language during a time of rapid forced <a href="/wiki/Japanization" title="Japanization">Japanization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[198]</a></sup> Today, both South Korea and North Korea celebrate Hangul Day as a national holiday.</p>

<li id="cite_note-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-35">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Grant, Reg G. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=s4njwZGrZg4C&amp;pg=PA104#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow"><i>1001 Battles That Changed the Course of World History</i></a>. <li id="cite_note-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-36">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Bedeski, Robert. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=iNl9AgAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA90#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow"><i>Human Security and the Chinese State: Historical Transformations and the Modern Quest for Sovereignty</i></a>. Routledge. p. 90. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781134125975" title="Special:BookSources/9781134125975">9781134125975</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">8 November</span> 2016</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AKorea&amp;rft.aufirst=Robert&amp;rft.aulast=Bedeski&amp;rft.btitle=Human+Security+and+the+Chinese+State%3A+Historical+Transformations+and+the+Modern+Quest+for+Sovereignty&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=9781134125975&amp;rft.pages=90&amp;rft.pub=Routledge&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DiNl9AgAAQBAJ%26pg%3DPA90%23v%3Donepage%26q%26f%3Dfalse&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Yi, Ki-baek. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=g2mdVwXpMzwC&amp;pg=PA47#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow"><i>A New History of Korea</i></a>. Harvard University Press. p. 47. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780674615762" title="Special:BookSources/9780674615762">9780674615762</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">29 July</span> 2016</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AKorea&amp;rft.aufirst=Ki-baek&amp;rft.aulast=Yi&amp;rft.btitle=A+New+History+of+Korea&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=9780674615762&amp;rft.pages=47&amp;rft.pub=Harvard+University+Press&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3Dg2mdVwXpMzwC%26pg%3DPA47%23v%3Donepage%26q%26f%3Dfalse&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> "Koguryŏ was the first to open hostilities, with a bold assault across the Liao River against Liao-hsi, in 598. The Sui emperor, Wen Ti, launched a retaliatory attack on Koguryŏ but met with reverses and turned back in mid-course. Yang Ti, the next Sui emperor, proceeded in 612 to mount an invasion of unprecedented magnitude, marshalling a huge force said to number over a million men. And when his armies failed to take Liao-tung Fortress (modern Liao-yang), the anchor of Koguryŏ's first line of defense, he had a nearly a third of his forces, some 300,000 strong, break off the battle there and strike directly at the Koguryŏ capital of P'yŏngyang. But the Sui army was lured into a trap by the famed Koguryŏ commander Ŭlchi Mundŏk, and suffered a calamitous defeat at the Salsu (Ch'ŏngch'ŏn) River. It is said that only 2,700 of the 300,000 Sui soldiers who had crossed the Yalu survived to find their way back, and the Sui emperor now lifted the siege of Liao-tung Fortress and withdrew his forces to China proper. Yang Ti continued to send his armies against Koguryŏ but again without success, and before long his war-weakened empire crumbled."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Nahm-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Nahm_38-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Nahm, Andrew C. (2005). <i>A Panorama of 5000 Years: Korean History</i> (Second revised ed.). Seoul: Hollym International Corporation. p. 18. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/093087868X" title="Special:BookSources/093087868X">093087868X</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AKorea&amp;rft.aufirst=Andrew+C.&amp;rft.aulast=Nahm&amp;rft.btitle=A+Panorama+of+5000+Years%3A+Korean+History&amp;rft.date=2005&amp;rft.edition=Second+revised&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=093087868X&amp;rft.pages=18&amp;rft.place=Seoul&amp;rft.pub=Hollym+International+Corporation&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> "China, which had been split into many states since the early 3rd century, was reunified by the Sui dynasty at the end of the 6th century. Soon after that, Sui China mobilized a large number of troops and launched war against Koguryŏ. However, the people of Koguryŏ were united and they were able to repel the Chinese aggressors. In 612, Sui troops invaded Korea again, but Koguryŏ forces fought bravely and destroyed Sui troops everywhere. General Ŭlchi Mundŏk of Koguryŏ completely wiped out some 300,000 Sui troops which came across the Yalu River in the battles near the Salsu River (now Ch'ŏngch'ŏn River) with his ingenious military tactics. Only 2,700 Sui troops were able to flee from Korea. The Sui dynasty, which wasted so much energy and manpower in aggressive wars against Koguryŏ, fell in 618."</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-69"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-69">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Reischauer, Edwin Oldfather. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books/about/Ennin_s_Travels_in_T_ang_China.html?id=HXqEAAAACAAJ" rel="nofollow"><i>Ennins Travels in Tang China</i></a>. John Wiley &amp; Sons Canada, Limited. pp. 276–283. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780471070535" title="Special:BookSources/9780471070535">9780471070535</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">21 July</span> 2016</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AKorea&amp;rft.aufirst=Edwin+Oldfather&amp;rft.aulast=Reischauer&amp;rft.btitle=Ennins+Travels+in+Tang+China&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=9780471070535&amp;rft.pages=276-283&amp;rft.pub=John+Wiley+%26+Sons+Canada%2C+Limited&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%2Fabout%2FEnnin_s_Travels_in_T_ang_China.html%3Fid%3DHXqEAAAACAAJ&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> "From what Ennin tells us, it seems that commerce between East China, Korea and Japan was, for the most part, in the hands of men from Silla. Here in the relatively dangerous waters on the eastern fringes of the world, they performed the same functions as did the traders of the placid Mediterranean on the western fringes. This is a historical fact of considerable significance but one which has received virtually no attention in the standard historical compilations of that period or in the modern books based on these sources. . . . While there were limits to the influence of the Koreans along the eastern coast of China, there can be no doubt of their dominance over the waters off these shores. . . . The days of Korean maritime dominance in the Far East actually were numbered, but in Ennin's time the men of Silla were still the masters of the seas in their part of the world."</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-Balhae-93"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Balhae_93-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Lee, Ki-Baik (1984). <i>A New History of Korea</i>. Cambridge, Massachusetts: Harvard University Press. p. 103. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/067461576X" title="Special:BookSources/067461576X">067461576X</a>. <q>When Parhae perished at the hands of the Khitan around this same time, much of its ruling class, who were of Koguryŏ descent, fled to Koryŏ. Wang Kŏn warmly welcomed them and generously gave them land. Along with bestowing the name Wang Kye ("Successor of the Royal Wang") on the Parhae crown prince, Tae Kwang-hyŏn, Wang Kŏn entered his name in the royal household register, thus clearly conveying the idea that they belonged to the same lineage, and also had rituals performed in honor of his progenitor. Thus Koryŏ achieved a true national unification that embraced not only the Later Three Kingdoms but even survivors of Koguryŏ lineage from the Parhae kingdom.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AKorea&amp;rft.aufirst=Ki-Baik&amp;rft.aulast=Lee&amp;rft.btitle=A+New+History+of+Korea&amp;rft.date=1984&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=067461576X&amp;rft.pages=103&amp;rft.place=Cambridge%2C+Massachusetts&amp;rft.pub=Harvard+University+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-117"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-117">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Lewis, James B. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=z2SQAgAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PT139#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow"><i>Frontier Contact Between Choson Korea and Tokugawa Japan</i></a>. Routledge. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781135795986" title="Special:BookSources/9781135795986">9781135795986</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">20 July</span> 2016</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AKorea&amp;rft.aufirst=James+B.&amp;rft.aulast=Lewis&amp;rft.btitle=Frontier+Contact+Between+Choson+Korea+and+Tokugawa+Japan&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=9781135795986&amp;rft.pub=Routledge&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3Dz2SQAgAAQBAJ%26pg%3DPT139%23v%3Donepage%26q%26f%3Dfalse&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> "Tribute trade was the oldest and most important component of the trade structure, not for its volume or content, but for its symbolism. Japanese brought items to "offer" to Korea and received in exchange "gifts" of higher value, since Korea was a greater land receiving supplicants. Koreans viewed tribute trade as a "burden" and a favor extended to needy islanders; the significance was diplomatic not economic."</span></li>

Retrieved <span class="nowrap">20 September</span> 2016</span>. <q>They later devised three different systems for writing Korean with Chinese characters: Hyangchal, Gukyeol and Idu. These systems were similar to those developed later in Japan and were probably used as models by the Japanese.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AKorea&amp;rft.atitle=Invest+Korea+Journal&amp;rft.date=2005-01-01&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.volume=23&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D00a2AAAAIAAJ&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-197"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-197">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal"><a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=WAlWAAAAYAAJ&amp;focus=searchwithinvolume&amp;q=%22The+writing+systems%2C+called+%22idu%22+and+%22hyangchal%2C%22+existed+several+hundred+years+before+Hangul%22" rel="nofollow">"Korea Now"</a>. <b>29</b>. Korea Herald. 1 July 2000<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">20 September</span> 2016</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AKorea&amp;rft.atitle=Korea+Now&amp;rft.date=2000-07-01&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.volume=29&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DWAlWAAAAYAAJ%26focus%3Dsearchwithinvolume%26q%3D%2522The%2Bwriting%2Bsystems%252C%2Bcalled%2B%2522idu%2522%2Band%2B%2522hyangchal%252C%2522%2Bexisted%2Bseveral%2Bhundred%2Byears%2Bbefore%2BHangul%2522&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
